history)</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#eee; text-align:center;">Topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div style="text-align:left">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_women_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of women in the United Kingdom">History of women</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Military history of the United Kingdom">Military history</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/16px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/24px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:United_Kingdom" title="Portal:United Kingdom">United Kingdom&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:History of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:History of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>When Britain emerged victorious from the Second World War, the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> came to power and created a comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>, with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> giving free healthcare to all British citizens, and other reforms to benefits. The <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, railways, heavy industry, and coal mining were all <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalised</a>. The most controversial issue was nationalisation of steel, which was profitable unlike the others. Economic recovery was slow, housing was in short supply, bread was rationed along with many necessities in short supply. It was an "age of austerity". American loans and Marshall Plan grants kept the economy afloat. India, Pakistan, Burma and Ceylon gained independence. Britain was a strong anti-Soviet factor in the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and helped found <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> in 1949.\n</p><p>The Labour Party introduced charges for NHS dental services and glasses in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> returned to power in 1951, accepting most of Labour\'s postwar reforms, but introduced prescription charges to the NHS in 1952 and denationalized steel in 1953. They presided over 13 years of economic recovery and stability. However the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> of 1956 demonstrated Britain was no longer a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">Malaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> were granted independence during this period. Labour returned to power under <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> in 1964 and oversaw a series of social reforms including the partial decriminalisation of homosexuality and abortion, the relaxing of divorce laws and the end of capital punishment. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> returned the Conservatives to power from 1970 to 1974, and oversaw the decimalisation of British currency, the accession of Britain to the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>, and the height of <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. In the wake of the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> and a miner\'s strike, Heath introduced the three-day working week to conserve power.\n</p><p>Labour made a return to power in 1974 but a series of strikes carried out by trade unions over the winter of 1978/79 (known as the <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>) paralysed the country and as Labour lost its majority in parliament, a general election was called in 1979 which took <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> to power and began 18 years of Conservative government. Victory in the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a> (1982) and the government\'s strong opposition to trade unions helped lead the Conservative Party to another three terms in government. Thatcher initially pursued <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">monetarist</a> policies and went on to privatise many of Britain\'s nationalised companies such as <a href="/wiki/British_Telecom" class="mw-redirect" title="British Telecom">British Telecom</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Gas_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="British Gas Corporation">British Gas Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Steel_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="British Steel Corporation">British Steel Corporation</a>. She kept the National Health Service.  The controversial <a href="/wiki/Community_Charge" class="mw-redirect" title="Community Charge">Community Charge</a> (commonly called the "Poll Tax"), used to fund local government was unpopular and the Conservatives removed Thatcher as Prime Minister in 1990.\n</p><p>Major replaced the Poll Tax with the <a href="/wiki/Council_Tax" title="Council Tax">Council Tax</a> and oversaw successful British involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>. Despite a recession, Major led the Conservatives to a surprise victory in 1992. The events of <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a> in 1992, party disunity over the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and several scandals involving Conservative politicians led to Labour under <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> winning a landslide election victory in 1997. Labour had shifted its policies closer to the political centre, under the new slogan \'New Labour\'. The Bank of England was given independence over monetary policy and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> were given a devolved <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">Welsh Assembly</a> respectively. A devolved power sharing <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Executive" title="Northern Ireland Executive">Northern Ireland Executive</a> was established in 1998, believed by many to be the end of The Troubles.\n</p><p>Blair led Britain into the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%932014)" class="mw-redirect" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x932014)">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a> before leaving office in 2007, when he was succeeded by his Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Late-2000s financial crisis">global recession in 2008\xe2\x80\x9310</a> led to Labour\'s defeat in the 2010 election. It was replaced by a Conservative-<a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a> coalition, headed by <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, that pursued a series of public spending cuts to reduce the budget deficit. In June 2016, the UK voted to leave the European Union. Conservatives replaced Cameron with <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" <span class="toctext">Gordon Brown (2007\xe2\x80\x9310)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Coalition_Government,_2010\xe2\x80\x9315"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Coalition Government, 2010\xe2\x80\x9315</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Conservative_Government,_2015\xe2\x80\x93present"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Conservative Government, 2015\xe2\x80\x93present</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#David_Cameron_(2015\xe2\x80\x9316)"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">David Cameron (2015&#8211;16)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Withdrawal_from_the_EU"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Withdrawal from the EU</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Newspapers_and_primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Newspapers and primary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span id="Labour_Government.2C_1945.E2.80.9351"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_Government,_1945\xe2\x80\x9351">Labour Government, 1945\xe2\x80\x9351</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Labour Government, 1945\xe2\x80\x9351">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Attlee_ministry" title="Attlee ministry">Attlee ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Attlee_BW_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Attlee_BW_cropped.jpg/220px-Attlee_BW_cropped.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Attlee_BW_cropped.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="225" data-file-height="267" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Attlee_BW_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, the landslide <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">1945 election</a> returned the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> to power and <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> became Prime Minister of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler19895_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler19895-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The party quickly nationalised critical sectors of the economy, especially declining industries. The <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> was <a href="/wiki/Nationalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalisation">nationalised</a> along with railways (see <a href="/wiki/Transport_Act_1947" title="Transport Act 1947">Transport Act 1947</a>), coal mining, public utilities and heavy industry. The most controversial case was the takeover of the highly profitable<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> iron and steel industry, which was opposed and finally reversed by the Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Welfare_state">Welfare state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Welfare state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_National_Health_Service" title="History of the National Health Service">History of the National Health Service</a></div>\n<p>A comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> was created with the <a href="/wiki/National_Insurance_Act_1946" title="National Insurance Act 1946">National Insurance Act 1946</a>, in which people in work paid a flat rate of <a href="/wiki/National_insurance" class="mw-redirect" title="National insurance">national insurance</a>. In return, they (and the wives of male contributors) were eligible for flat-rate <a href="/wiki/Pension" title="Pension">pensions</a>, sickness benefit, unemployment benefit, and funeral benefit. Various other pieces of legislation provided for child benefit and support for people with no other source of income.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the estimation of historians, and later politicians of the major parties, the most successful and permanent program was the creation of a <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service_(England)" title="National Health Service (England)">National Health Service</a> which started operations in 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It entitled all citizens to healthcare, which, funded by taxation, was free at the point of delivery. The opposition from physicians was bought off by allowing them to keep lucrative private practices on the side.  All hospitals were nationalized and brought into the system. John Carrier and Ian Kendall find that the mission for Minister of Health <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a> was resolving "The potential conflict between the aim of providing a universalist, comprehensive health service of a good standard and that of containing health costs to a reasonable level, and how to finance the system in such a way that certainty and sufficiency of funds could be guaranteed."<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a> adds that Bevan had to persuade "the most conservative and respected profession in the country to accept and operate the Labour government\'s most intrinsically socialist proposition.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>  In the end historians give Bevan the major credit for the success.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>One of the main achievements of Attlee\'s government was the maintenance of near full employment. The government maintained most of the wartime controls over the economy, including control over the allocation of materials and manpower, and unemployment rarely rose above 500,000, or 3% of the total workforce. In fact labour <i>shortages</i> proved to be more of a problem. One area where the government was not quite as successful was in housing, which was also the responsibility of Aneurin Bevan. The government had a target to build 400,000 new houses a year to replace those which had been destroyed in the war, but shortages of materials and manpower meant that less than half this number were built.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_affairs">Foreign affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Foreign affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>For decades the Conservatives were split on India between die-hard imperialists (led by Churchill) and moderate elements who tried to provide limited local control.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Meanwhile, the small Labour minority in Parliament was sympathetic to the Congress movement led by <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jawaharial_Nehru" class="mw-redirect" title="Jawaharial Nehru">Jawaharial Nehru</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Decolonisation was never a major election issue; Labour was not officially in favour of decolonisation when it was elected in 1945. With violence escalating in India after the war, but with British financial power at a low ebb, large-scale military involvement was impossible. The Viceroy of India warned he needed a further seven army divisions to prevent communal violence if independence negotiations failed. None were available, so political restructuring was accelerated.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The Labour government gave independence to India and Pakistan in an unexpectedly quick move in 1947. Historian and Conservative party sympathiser <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Roberts_(historian)" title="Andrew Roberts (historian)">Andrew Roberts</a> says the independence of India was a "national humiliation" but it was necessitated by urgent financial, administrative, strategic and political needs.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  Whereas Churchill in 1940-45 had tightened the hold on India and imprisoned the Congress leadership, Labour had looked forward to making it a fully independent dominion like Canada or Australia. Many of the Congress leaders in India had studied in England, and were highly regarded as fellow idealistic socialists by Labour leaders. Attlee was the Labour expert on India and took special charge of decolonization.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  Attlee found that Churchill\'s viceroy, <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Wavell,_1st_Earl_Wavell" title="Archibald Wavell, 1st Earl Wavell">Field Marshal Wavell</a>, was too imperialistic, too keen on military solutions (he wanted seven more Army divisions) and too neglectful of Indian political alignments.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The new Viceroy was <a href="/wiki/Louis_Mountbatten,_1st_Earl_Mountbatten_of_Burma" title="Louis Mountbatten, 1st Earl Mountbatten of Burma">Lord Mountbatten</a>, the dashing  war hero and a cousin of the King.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The boundary between the newly created states of Pakistan and India involved the widespread resettlement of millions of Muslims and Hindus (and many Sikhs). Extreme violence ensued when Punjab and Bengal provinces were split. Historian Yasmin Khan estimates that between a half-million and a million men, women and children were killed.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Gandhi himself was assassinated by a Hindu activist in January 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_relations">International relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: International relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Britain became a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> during this time and of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> in 1949. Under foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bevin" title="Ernest Bevin">Ernest Bevin</a>, Britain took a strong anti-Soviet position in the emerging <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Cooperation with the United States was good, except in the area of nuclear weapons, where president <a href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Truman">Harry Truman</a> <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Agreement#End_of_the_Quebec_Agreement" title="Quebec Agreement">ended cooperation</a>. Britain had to develop its own nuclear arsenal, with <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hurricane" title="Operation Hurricane">the first test</a> in 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>  Mandatory military service continued, as despite the end of WWII, Britain continued to wage numerous small conflicts around the globe: the <a href="/wiki/Malayan_Emergency" title="Malayan Emergency">Malayan Emergency</a>, 1948-1960,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> in Kenya against the <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</a> (1952\xe2\x80\x9360) and against Egypt in the 1956 <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Finances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-American_loan" title="Anglo-American loan">Anglo-American loan</a></div>\n<p>International finance was a troublesome issue, as Britain had used up all its reserves and had to borrow large sums from the United States and from the International Monetary Fund.  The U.S. provided  <a href="/wiki/Anglo-American_loan" title="Anglo-American loan">a loan of $3.75 billion (US$57 billion in 2017) at a low 2% interest rate</a>; Canada loaned an additional US$1.19 billion (US$16 billion in 2017).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Starting in 1948, the United States provided grants of $3.3 billion (about US$33 billion in 2017). These funds came through the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> and did not have to be repaid, however they carried the proviso that Britain modernize the management of its major corporations. The aid permitted Britain to provide consumption at tolerable levels despite the austerity.  About 40 per cent of the dollars went for food, drink and tobacco from the U.S. and 40 per cent on raw materials. The remainder went mostly for machinery and oil.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1950, the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> caused a new heavy drain on the Treasury for military expenses. This caused the bitter split inside the Labour party.  The Conservatives made austerity a major issue in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1950" title="United Kingdom general election, 1950">general election of 1950</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Labour lost most of its large majority. The swing was 3.6% against it and it lost 78 seats, leaving Attlee with a slim majority in the House.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> However, a year later Labour lost the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">general election of 1951</a> despite polling more votes than in the 1945 election, and indeed more votes than the Conservative Party.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Conservative_Government.2C_1951.E2.80.9364"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_Government,_1951\xe2\x80\x9364">Conservative Government, 1951\xe2\x80\x9364</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Conservative Government, 1951\xe2\x80\x9364">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Winston_Churchill_.281951.E2.80.9355.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Winston_Churchill_(1951\xe2\x80\x9355)">Winston Churchill (1951\xe2\x80\x9355)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Winston Churchill (1951\xe2\x80\x9355)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Third_Churchill_ministry" title="Third Churchill ministry">Third Churchill ministry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Later_life_of_Winston_Churchill" title="Later life of Winston Churchill">Later life of Winston Churchill</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg/220px-Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg/330px-Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg/440px-Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="1461" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Winston_Churchill_cph.3a49758.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg/170px-Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg/255px-Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg/340px-Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg 2x" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="751" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_and_Philip_1953.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newly crowned Queen <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Prince_Philip" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Philip">Prince Philip</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> again became Prime Minister.  His third government \xe2\x80\x94 after the wartime national government and the short caretaker government of 1945 \xe2\x80\x94 would last until his resignation in 1955. During this period he renewed what he called the "special relationship" between Britain and the United States, and engaged himself in the formation of the post-war order.\n</p><p>His domestic priorities were, however, overshadowed by a series of foreign policy crises, which were partly the result of the continued decline of British military and imperial prestige and power.  Being a strong proponent of Britain as an international power, Churchill would often meet such moments with direct action.\n</p><p>In February 1952, <a href="/wiki/George_VI_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George VI of the United Kingdom">King George VI</a> died and was succeeded by his eldest daughter <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth</a>. Her <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">coronation</a> on 2 June 1953 gave the British people a renewed sense of national pride and enthusiasm which had been lowered by the war. Elizabeth II has now been the monarch for 66&#160;years, 258&#160;days. She celebrated her <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jubilee_of_Elizabeth_II" title="Diamond Jubilee of Elizabeth II">diamond jubilee</a>, marking 60 years since her ascension to the throne, in 2012.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-Iranian_Oil_Dispute">Anglo-Iranian Oil Dispute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Anglo-Iranian Oil Dispute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In March 1951, the Iranian parliament (the Majlis) voted to nationalise the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Iranian_Oil_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Iranian Oil Company">Anglo-Iranian Oil Company</a> (AIOC) and its holdings by passing a bill strongly backed by <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Mosaddegh" title="Mohammad Mosaddegh">Mohammad Mosaddegh</a>, who was elected Prime Minister the following April by a large majority of the parliament.  The International Court of Justice was called in to settle the dispute, but a 50/50 profit-sharing arrangement, with recognition of nationalisation, was rejected by Mossadegh. Direct negotiations between the British and the Iranian government ceased, and over the course of 1951, the British ratcheted up the pressure on the Iranian government and explored the possibility of a coup against it. U.S. President Harry S. Truman was reluctant to agree, placing a much higher priority on the Korean War.\nChurchill\'s return to power and Eisenhower\'s presidency brought with them a policy of undermining the Mossadegh government. Both sides floated proposals unacceptable to the other, each side believing that time was on its side.  Negotiations broke down, and as the blockade\'s political and economic costs mounted inside Iran, <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d&#39;\xc3\xa9tat">coup plots</a> arose from the army and pro-British factions in the Majlis.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Mau_Mau_Rebellion">The Mau Mau Rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Mau Mau Rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</a></div>\n<p>In 1951, grievances against the colonial distribution of land came to a head with the Kenya Africa Union demanding greater representation and land reform.  When these demands were rejected, more radical elements came forward, launching the Mau Mau rebellion in 1952. On 17 August 1952, a state of emergency was declared, and British troops were flown to <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> to deal with the rebellion. As both sides increased the ferocity of their attacks, the country moved to full-scale civil war.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malayan_Emergency">Malayan Emergency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Malayan Emergency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Malayan_Emergency" title="Malayan Emergency">Malayan Emergency</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Malaya" title="Federation of Malaya">Malaya</a>, a rebellion against British rule had been in progress since 1948, led by Communists based in the local Chinese community.  Once again, Churchill\'s government inherited a crisis, and once again Churchill chose to use direct military action against those in rebellion while attempting to build an alliance with those who were not. He stepped up the implementation of a "hearts and minds" campaign and approved the creation of fortified villages, a tactic that would become a recurring part of Western military strategy in South-East Asia, especially in the American role in the  <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Anthony_Eden_.281955.E2.80.9357.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthony_Eden_(1955\xe2\x80\x9357)">Anthony Eden (1955\xe2\x80\x9357)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Anthony Eden (1955\xe2\x80\x9357)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Eden_ministry" title="Eden ministry">Eden ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthony_Eden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Anthony_Eden.jpg/220px-Anthony_Eden.jpg" width="220" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Anthony_Eden.jpg/330px-Anthony_Eden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Anthony_Eden.jpg/440px-Anthony_Eden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anthony_Eden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Anthony Eden</a></div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suez_crisis">Suez crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Suez crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Suez_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Suez crisis">Suez crisis</a></div>\n<p>In April 1955, Churchill finally retired, and <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Sir Anthony Eden</a> succeeded him as Prime Minister. Eden was a very popular figure, as a result of his long wartime service and also his famous good looks and charm. On taking office he immediately called a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1955" title="United Kingdom general election, 1955">general election</a>, at which the Conservatives were returned with an increased majority. He left domestic issues to his lieutenants such as <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a>, and concentrated largely on foreign policy, forming a close alliance with US President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 26 July 1956 <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, President of Egypt, nationalised the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> company, in violation of the international agreement he had signed with the UK in 1954. It had been owned and controlled by Britain since 1875 and was seen as essential to national defence and access to the Far East. Eden drawing on his experience in the 1930s, saw Nasser as another <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Mussolini</a> who had to be stopped. In November 1956, after months of negotiation and attempts at mediation had failed to dissuade Nasser, Britain and France, in conjunction with Israel, invaded Egypt and occupied the Suez Canal Zone.\n</p><p>Eisenhower had warned Eden not to do it, saying the American people would never approve of a military solution to the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> He threatened to use financial pressure unless the British withdrew from Egypt. Eden had ignored Britain\'s financial dependence on the US in the wake of World War II, and was forced to bow to American pressure to withdraw. Eden had poor staff support because the Foreign Office, Commonwealth Relations Office, and Colonial Office had been slow to realize the need for change in Britain\'s world role. After Suez they started to heed Treasury warnings about the effect of high defence expenditure on the economy, and the slow growth of the British population compared with the United States and the Soviet Union. Historians often use the crisis to mark the end of Britain\'s status as a superpower, being able to act and control international affairs without assistance or coalition.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Harold_Macmillan_.281957.E2.80.9363.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Harold_Macmillan_(1957\xe2\x80\x9363)">Harold Macmillan (1957\xe2\x80\x9363)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Harold Macmillan (1957\xe2\x80\x9363)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Conservative_government_1957%E2%80%9364" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative government 1957\xe2\x80\x9364">Conservative government 1957&#8211;64</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-1-17(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-1-17%28cropped%29.jpg" width="198" height="198" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="198" data-file-height="198" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-1-17(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Eden resigned in the wake of the Suez Crisis, and his Chancellor of the Exchequer, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> succeeded him as Prime Minister on 10 January. He brought the economic concerns of the exchequer into the premiership, but his approach to the economy was to seek high employment; whereas his treasury ministers argued that to support the Bretton-Wood\'s requirement on the pound sterling would require strict control of the money base, and hence a rise in unemployment. Their advice was rejected and in January 1958, all the Treasury ministers resigned. Macmillan brushed aside this incident as \'a little local difficulty\'.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Macmillan wanted the new National Incomes Commission to institute controls on income as part of his growth without inflation policy; it failed when the unions boycotted it.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of Macmillan\'s more noteworthy actions was the <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_Kingdom#After_1945" title="Conscription in the United Kingdom">end of conscription</a>. National Service ended gradually from 1957; in November 1960 the last men entered service.\nWith British youth no longer subject to military service and with postwar rationing and reconstruction ended, the stage was set for the social uprisings of the 1960s to commence.\n</p><p>Macmillan took close control of foreign policy. He worked to narrow the post-Suez rift with the US, where his wartime friendship with <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> was useful, and the two had a pleasant conference in Bermuda as early as March 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  The amiable relationship continued with President John F. Kennedy after 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\nMacmillan also saw the value of a rapprochement with Europe and sought entry to the Common Market. In terms of the Empire, Macmillan continued <a href="/wiki/Decolonization#Decolonization_after_1945" title="Decolonization">decolonisation</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Wind_of_Change_speech" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind of Change speech">Wind of Change speech</a> in February 1960 indicating his policy. <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Malaya" title="Federation of Malaya">Malaya</a> were granted independence in 1957, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> in 1960 and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> in 1963.  However, in the Middle East Macmillan ensured Britain remained a force \xe2\x80\x94 intervening over <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> in 1958 (<a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">14 July Revolution</a>) and 1960 and becoming involved in <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>. Immigrants from the Commonwealth flocked to England after The British Government posted invitations in the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a>, for Workers to come to England to "help the mother Country".\n</p><p>He led the Tories to victory in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="United Kingdom general election, 1959">October 1959 general election</a>, increasing his party\'s majority from 67 to 107 seats.\n</p><p>Following the technical failures of a British independent nuclear deterrent with the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Streak_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Streak missile">Blue Streak</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Steel_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Steel missile">Blue Steel</a> projects, Macmillan negotiated the supply of American <a href="/wiki/Polaris_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Polaris missile">Polaris missiles</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Nassau_agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Nassau agreement">Nassau agreement</a> in December 1962.  Previously he had agreed to base sixty <a href="/wiki/Thor_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Thor missile">Thor missiles</a> in Britain under joint control, and since late 1957 the American <a href="/wiki/McMahon_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="McMahon Act">McMahon Act</a> had been eased to allow Britain more access to nuclear technology. Britain, the US, and the Soviet Union signed the <a href="/wiki/Partial_Test_Ban_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Partial Test Ban Treaty">Partial Test Ban Treaty</a> in autumn 1963. Britain\'s application to join the Common Market was vetoed by French President <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> on 29 January 1963, in due to his fear that \'the end would be a colossal Atlantic Community dependent on America\', and personal anger at the Anglo-American nuclear deal.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_payments" title="Balance of payments">balance of payments</a> problems led to the imposition of a seven-month <a href="/wiki/Wage_freeze" class="mw-redirect" title="Wage freeze">wage freeze</a> in 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-papers_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papers-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>   This caused the government to lose popularity and led to a series of <a href="/wiki/By-election" title="By-election">by-election</a> defeats. He organised <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Government_1957%E2%80%9364#July_1962_\xe2\x80\x93_October_1963" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Government 1957\xe2\x80\x9364">a major Cabinet change in July 1962</a> but he continued to lose support from within his party.\n</p><p>He <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan#Resignation" title="Harold Macmillan">resigned on 18 October 1963</a> after he had been admitted to hospital for <a href="/wiki/Prostate" title="Prostate">prostate</a> trouble. He died 23 years later, in 1986.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Alec_Douglas-Home_.281963.E2.80.9364.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Alec_Douglas-Home_(1963\xe2\x80\x9364)">Alec Douglas-Home (1963\xe2\x80\x9364)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Alec Douglas-Home (1963\xe2\x80\x9364)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alec_Douglas-Home_(c1963).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Alec_Douglas-Home_%28c1963%29.jpg/220px-Alec_Douglas-Home_%28c1963%29.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Alec_Douglas-Home_%28c1963%29.jpg/330px-Alec_Douglas-Home_%28c1963%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Alec_Douglas-Home_%28c1963%29.jpg/440px-Alec_Douglas-Home_%28c1963%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1737" data-file-height="2316" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alec_Douglas-Home_(c1963).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Macmillan\'s successor was the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Home" title="Earl of Home">Earl of Home</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a>. However, as no prime minister had led from the House of Lords since the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Marquess of Salisbury</a> in 1902, Home chose to become a member of parliament so he could enter the House of Commons. He disclaimed his earldom and, as "Sir Alec Douglas-Home", contested a <a href="/wiki/By-election" title="By-election">by-election</a> in the safe seat of <a href="/wiki/Kinross_and_Western_Perthshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Kinross and Western Perthshire (UK Parliament constituency)">Kinross &amp; West Perthshire</a>. He won and is the only prime minister to resign the Lords to enter the Commons.  His demeanor and appearance remained aristocratic and old-fashioned, however. His understanding of economics was primitive, and he gave his chancellor <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Maudling" title="Reginald Maudling">Reginald Maudling</a> <a href="/wiki/Rein" title="Rein">free rein</a> to handle financial affairs. Home\'s few domestic policies were not well received, but he did abolish retail price maintenance, which allowed consumers to find more bargains on sale.  He enjoyed dealing with foreign policy, but there were no major crises or issues to resolve. His Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a> was not especially energetic.  Britain\'s application to join Europe had already been vetoed by de Gaulle, the Cuban missile crisis had been resolved, and Berlin was again on the back burner. Decolonization issues were largely routine, and the Rhodesia and South African crises lay in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1964" title="United Kingdom general election, 1964">1964 general election</a>, the Labour Party was returned to power under <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>. Douglas-Home became Leader of the Opposition. In July 1965, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> defeated Reginald Maudling and <a href="/wiki/Enoch_Powell" title="Enoch Powell">Enoch Powell</a> to succeed him as Conservative Party leader. Enoch Powell was given the post of Shadow Defence Secretary and became a figure of national prominence when he made the controversial <a href="/wiki/Rivers_of_Blood_speech" title="Rivers of Blood speech">Rivers of Blood speech</a> in 1968, warning on the dangers of mass <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom_since_1922" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration to the United Kingdom since 1922">immigration from Commonwealth nations</a>. It is possible that the Conservatives\' success in the 1970 general election was a result of the large public following Powell attained, even as he was sacked from the shadow cabinet.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Labour_Government.2C_1964.E2.80.9370"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_Government,_1964\xe2\x80\x9370">Labour Government, 1964\xe2\x80\x9370</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Labour Government, 1964\xe2\x80\x9370">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/First_Wilson_ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="First Wilson ministry">First Wilson ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Harold_Wilson_(1967).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Harold_Wilson_%281967%29.jpg/220px-Harold_Wilson_%281967%29.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Harold_Wilson_%281967%29.jpg/330px-Harold_Wilson_%281967%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Harold_Wilson_%281967%29.jpg/440px-Harold_Wilson_%281967%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2021" data-file-height="2695" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Harold_Wilson_(1967).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harold_Wilson">Harold Wilson</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Harold Wilson">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1964, Labour regained the premiership, as <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> narrowly won the general election with a majority of five. This was not sufficient to last for a full term and, after a short period of competent government, in March 1966, he won re-election with a landslide majority of 99. As Prime Minister, his opponents accused him of deviousness, especially over the matter of devaluation of the pound in November 1967. Wilson had rejected devaluation for many years, yet in his broadcast had seemed to present it as a triumph.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Open_University">Open University</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Open University">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Wilson regarded with special pride setting up  the <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a> as a model for Europe. Plans were  drafted by Jennie Lee, the Minister for the Arts; she had Wilson\'s full support.  He saw the Open University as a major marker of the Labour Party\'s commitment to modernisation. He emphasized that it would strengthen a more competitive economy while also fostering greater equality of opportunity and social mobility. He especially favoured heavy use of technology, such as television and radio broadcast of its courses.  There were strong doubters and opponents in the  government and in commercial broadcasting; Wilson outmaneuvered them to get the budget approved, even though its sums proved far too small.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam_War">Vietnam War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Vietnam War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Overseas, Wilson was troubled by crises in  <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. The Vietnam War was a delicate issue, as President Lyndon Johnson urdently needed a symbolic British military presence. "Lyndon Johnson is begging me even to send a bagpipe band to Vietnam," Wilson told his Cabinet in December 1964. Labour decided not to antagonize its strong antiwar element and refused Johnson\'s pleas.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> However, Wilson provided the Americans with intelligence, military weapons, and jungle training, and allowed some 2000 British soldiers to volunteer for service in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_strife">Industrial strife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Industrial strife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/In_Place_of_Strife" title="In Place of Strife">In Place of Strife</a></div>\n<p>In addition to the damage done to its reputation by devaluation, Wilson\'s Government suffered from the perception that its response to industrial relations problems was inadequate. A six-week strike of members of the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Seamen" title="National Union of Seamen">National Union of Seamen</a>, which began shortly after Wilson\' re-election in 1966, did much to reinforce this perception, along with Wilson\'s own sense of insecurity in office.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Conservative_Government.2C_1970.E2.80.9374"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_Government,_1970\xe2\x80\x9374">Conservative Government, 1970\xe2\x80\x9374</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Conservative Government, 1970\xe2\x80\x9374">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Heath_ministry" title="Heath ministry">Heath ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heathdod.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Heathdod.JPG/220px-Heathdod.JPG" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Heathdod.JPG/330px-Heathdod.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Heathdod.JPG/440px-Heathdod.JPG 2x" data-file-width="581" data-file-height="708" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heathdod.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_Heath">Edward Heath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Edward Heath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The premiership of his successor <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Sir Edward Heath</a> was the bloodiest in the history of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> <a href="/wiki/Troubles" class="mw-redirect" title="Troubles">Troubles</a>. He was prime minister at the time of <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1972)" title="Bloody Sunday (1972)">Bloody Sunday</a> in 1972 when 14 unarmed men were killed by British soldiers during a banned <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> march in <a href="/wiki/Derry" title="Derry">Derry</a>. In 2003, he gave evidence to the Saville Inquiry and claimed that he never promoted or agreed to the use of unlawful lethal force in Northern Ireland. In July 1972, he permitted his <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Secretary of State for Northern Ireland">Secretary of State for Northern Ireland</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Whitelaw" class="mw-redirect" title="William Whitelaw">William Whitelaw</a> to hold unofficial talks in London with a <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> delegation by <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_Mac_Stiof%C3%A1in" class="mw-redirect" title="Se\xc3\xa1n Mac Stiof\xc3\xa1in">Se\xc3\xa1n Mac Stiof\xc3\xa1in</a>. In the aftermath of these unsuccessful talks, the Heath government pushed for a peaceful settlement with the democratic political parties. In 1974, the <a href="/wiki/Sunningdale_Agreement" title="Sunningdale Agreement">Sunningdale Agreement</a> was produced but fiercely repudiated by many Unionists and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a> ceased to support the Conservatives at Westminster.\n</p><p>Heath took Britain into the Common Market (later renamed The European Union and again renamed The EEC) on 1 January 1973 after winning the decisive vote in the House by 336-244. It was, says biographer John Campbell, "Heath\'s finest hour."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, on the domestic front, galloping inflation led him into confrontation with some of the most powerful trade unions. Energy shortages related to the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">oil shock</a> resulted in much of the country\'s industry working a <a href="/wiki/Three-Day_Week" title="Three-Day Week">Three-Day Week</a> to conserve power. In an attempt to bolster his government, Heath called an election for 28 February 1974. The result was inconclusive: the Conservative Party received a plurality of votes cast, but the Labour Party gained a plurality of seats due to the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist</a> MPs refusing to support the Conservatives. Heath began negotiations with leaders of the Liberal Party to form a coalition, but, when these failed, resigned as Prime Minister.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Labour_Government.2C_1974.E2.80.9379"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_Government,_1974\xe2\x80\x9379">Labour Government, 1974\xe2\x80\x9379</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Labour Government, 1974\xe2\x80\x9379">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Labour_government_1974%E2%80%9379" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour government 1974\xe2\x80\x9379">Labour government 1974&#8211;79</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Harold_Wilson_.281974.E2.80.9376.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Harold_Wilson_(1974\xe2\x80\x9376)">Harold Wilson (1974\xe2\x80\x9376)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Harold Wilson (1974\xe2\x80\x9376)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Heath was replaced by Harold Wilson, who returned on 4 March 1974 to form a minority government. Wilson was confirmed in office, with a three-seat majority, in a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_October_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, October 1974">second election in October of the same year</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  It was a manifesto pledge in the general election of February 1974 for a Labour government to re-negotiate better terms for Britain in the EEC, and then hold a referendum on whether Britain should stay in the EEC on the new terms. After the House of Commons voted in favour of retaining the Common Market on the renegotiated terms, a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_referendum,_1975" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom referendum, 1975">referendum</a> was held on 5 June 1975. A majority were in favour of retaining the Common Market.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  But Wilson was not able to end the economic crisis. Unemployment remained well in excess of 1,000,000, inflation peaked at 24% in 1975, and the national debt was increasing. The rise of punk rock bands such as the <a href="/wiki/Sex_Pistols" title="Sex Pistols">Sex Pistols</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Clash" title="The Clash">The Clash</a> were a reflection of the discontent felt by British youth during the difficulties of the late 1970s.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="James_Callaghan_.281976.E2.80.9379.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="James_Callaghan_(1976\xe2\x80\x9379)">James Callaghan (1976\xe2\x80\x9379)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: James Callaghan (1976\xe2\x80\x9379)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Callaghan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/James_Callaghan.JPG/220px-James_Callaghan.JPG" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/James_Callaghan.JPG/330px-James_Callaghan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/James_Callaghan.JPG/440px-James_Callaghan.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1144" data-file-height="1468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Callaghan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Wilson announced his surprise resignation on 16 March 1976 and unofficially endorsed his Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> as his successor. His broad popularity in many parts of the Labour movement saw him through <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1976" title="Labour Party (UK) leadership election, 1976">three ballots</a> of Labour MPs, defeating the arch-Bevanite <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a>, the main left-wing candidate. Callaghan was the first Prime Minister to have held all three leading Cabinet positions \xe2\x80\x94 Chancellor of the Exchequer, Home Secretary and Foreign Secretary \xe2\x80\x94 prior to becoming Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Callaghan\'s support for and from the union movement should not be mistaken for a left wing position. Callaghan continued Wilson\'s policy of a balanced Cabinet and relied heavily on Michael Foot. Foot was made <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_House_of_Commons" title="Leader of the House of Commons">Leader of the House of Commons</a> and given the task of steering through the government\'s legislative programme.\n</p><p>Callaghan\'s time as Prime Minister was dominated by the troubles in running a Government with a minority in the <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a>; by-election defeats had wiped out Labour\'s three-seat majority by early 1977. Callaghan was forced to make deals with minor parties in order to survive, including the <a href="/wiki/Lib-Lab_pact" class="mw-redirect" title="Lib-Lab pact">Lib-Lab pact</a>. He had been forced to accept referendums on devolution in Scotland and Wales (the first went in favour but did not reach the required majority, and the second went heavily against).\n</p><p>However, by the autumn of 1978 the economy was showing signs of recovery \xe2\x80\x93 although unemployment now stood at 1,500,000, economic growth was strong and inflation had fallen below 10%.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Most opinion polls were showing Labour ahead and he was expected to call an election before the end of the year. His decision not to has been described as the biggest mistake of his premiership.\n</p><p>Callaghan\'s way of dealing with the long-term economic difficulties involved pay restraint which had been operating for four years with reasonable success. He gambled that a fifth year would further improve the economy and allow him to be re-elected in 1979, and so attempted to hold pay rises to 5% or less. The Trade Unions rejected continued pay restraint and in a succession of strikes over the winter of 1978/79 (known as the <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a>) secured higher pay, although it had virtually paralysed the country, tarnished Britain\'s political reputation and seen the Conservatives surge ahead in the opinion polls.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He was forced to call an election when the House of Commons passed a <a href="/wiki/Motion_of_No_Confidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Motion of No Confidence">Motion of No Confidence</a> by one vote on 28 March 1979. The Conservatives, with advertising consultants <a href="/wiki/Saatchi_and_Saatchi" class="mw-redirect" title="Saatchi and Saatchi">Saatchi and Saatchi</a>, ran a campaign on the slogan "Labour isn\'t working." As expected, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (who had succeeded Edward Heath as Conservative leader in <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1975" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1975">February 1975</a>) won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">general election</a> held on 3 May, becoming Britain\'s first female prime minister.\n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O._Morgan" title="Kenneth O. Morgan">Kenneth O. Morgan</a> states:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The fall of James Callahan in the summer of 1979 meant, according to most commentators across the political spectrum, the end of an <i>ancien r\xc3\xa9gime,</i> a system of corporatism, Keynesian spending programmes, subsidized welfare, and trade union power.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Historians Alan Sked and Chris Cook have summarised the general consensus of historians regarding Labour in power in the 1970s:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If Wilson\'s record as prime minister was soon felt to have been one of failure, that sense of failure was powerfully reinforced by Callahan\'s term as premier. Labour, it seemed, was incapable of positive achievements. It was unable to control inflation, unable to control the unions, unable to solve the Irish problem, unable to solve the Rhodesian question, unable to secure its proposals for Welsh and Scottish devolution, unable to reach a popular <i>modus vivendi</i> with the Common Market, unable even to maintain itself in power until it could go to the country and the date of its own choosing. It was little wonder, therefore, that Mrs. Thatcher resoundingly defeated it in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h2><span id="Conservative_Government.2C_1979.E2.80.9397"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_Government,_1979\xe2\x80\x9397">Conservative Government, 1979\xe2\x80\x9397</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Conservative Government, 1979\xe2\x80\x9397">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Margaret_Thatcher_.281979.E2.80.9390.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Margaret_Thatcher_(1979\xe2\x80\x9390)">Margaret Thatcher (1979\xe2\x80\x9390)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Margaret Thatcher (1979\xe2\x80\x9390)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Premiership of Margaret Thatcher">Premiership of Margaret Thatcher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thatcher_ministry_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Thatcher ministry (disambiguation)">Thatcher ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_(1983).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Margaret_Thatcher_%281983%29.jpg/220px-Margaret_Thatcher_%281983%29.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Margaret_Thatcher_%281983%29.jpg/330px-Margaret_Thatcher_%281983%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Margaret_Thatcher_%281983%29.jpg/440px-Margaret_Thatcher_%281983%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2444" data-file-height="3259" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Margaret_Thatcher_(1983).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Thatcher formed a government on 4 May 1979, with a mandate to reverse the UK\'s economic decline and to reduce the role of the state in the economy. Thatcher was incensed by one contemporary view within the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Service_(United_Kingdom)" title="Civil Service (United Kingdom)">Civil Service</a> that its job was to manage the UK\'s decline from the days of Empire, and wanted the country to punch above its weight in international affairs. She was a philosophic soulmate with <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, elected in 1980 in the United States, and to a lesser extent, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Mulroney" title="Brian Mulroney">Brian Mulroney</a>, who was elected in 1984 in Canada. It seemed for a time that conservatism might be the dominant political philosophy in the major English-speaking nations for the era.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Irish_issues">Irish issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Irish issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army_actions_(1980%E2%80%9389)" title="Chronology of Provisional Irish Republican Army actions (1980\xe2\x80\x9389)">Chronology of Provisional Irish Republican Army actions (1980\xe2\x80\x9389)</a></div>\n<p>Northern Ireland was in a violent phase.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Insurgents planted bombs and assassinated its foes, including in 1979 <a href="/wiki/Airey_Neave" title="Airey Neave">Airey Neave</a>, Thatcher\'s close friend who was slated to take charge there. The <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> (INLA), a republican paramilitary group, claimed responsibility. On 27 August 1979, the IRA Assassinated <a href="/wiki/Louis_Mountbatten,_1st_Earl_Mountbatten_of_Burma" title="Louis Mountbatten, 1st Earl Mountbatten of Burma">Lord Mountbatten</a>, a member of the royal family, and other units killed 18 policeman.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Sheer luck on the early morning of 12 October 1984 saved Thatcher\'s life as five were killed by a bomb planted by the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brighton_hotel_bombing" title="Brighton hotel bombing">Brighton\'s Grand Hotel</a> during the Conservative Party conference.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1981, a new tactic was used to mobilize support, as Provisional IRA and <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> prisoners went on <a href="/wiki/1981_Irish_hunger_strike" title="1981 Irish hunger strike">hunger strikes</a> to claim legitimacy for their cause in the act of making the ultimate sacrifice.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  It was a historic tactic that was revived for maximum impact, although it was attacked by the major news media and denounced by
