Catholic bishops.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thatcher continued the policy of "Ulsterisation" promoted by the previous Labour government, believing that the <a href="/wiki/Unionism_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unionism (Ireland)">unionists</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> should be at the forefront in combating <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">Irish republicanism</a>. This meant relieving the burden on the mainstream <a href="/wiki/British_army" class="mw-redirect" title="British army">British army</a> and elevating the role of the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Regiment" title="Ulster Defence Regiment">Ulster Defence Regiment</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1985, Thatcher signed the Hillsborough <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Agreement" title="Anglo-Irish Agreement">Anglo-Irish Agreement</a>, bringing the Dublin government into the peace process.  P. J. McLoughlin finds the consensus of scholars is that it was a significant factor contributing to the development of the Northern Ireland peace process.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>   However, the agreement was greeted with fury by Irish unionists. The <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionists" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Unionists">Ulster Unionists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionists</a> made an electoral pact and on 23 January 1986, staged an ad-hoc referendum by re-fighting their seats in by-elections, and won with one seat lost to the nationalist <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_and_Labour_Party" title="Social Democratic and Labour Party">Social Democratic and Labour Party</a> (SDLP). Then, unlike the Sunningdale Agreement in 1974, they found they could not bring the agreement down by a general strike. This was another effect of the changed balance of power in industrial relations. The Hillsborough agreement stood, and Thatcher punished the unionists for their non-cooperation by abolishing the devolved assembly she had created only four years before.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In economic policy, Thatcher and her Chancellor Sir <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a> started out with policies, including higher interest rates, to <a href="/wiki/Monetarism" title="Monetarism">drive down the rate of growth of the money supply</a>. She had a preference for indirect taxation over taxes on income: in 1979 <a href="/wiki/Exchange_Control" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange Control">exchange controls</a> were abolished and the top rate of income tax on "unearned" income cut from 98% to 60%, but <a href="/wiki/Value_added_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Value added tax">value added tax</a> (VAT) was increased sharply to 15% with the result that inflation also rose. These moves hit businesses, especially in the manufacturing sector, and <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> \xe2\x80\x93 which had stood at 1,500,000 by the time of the 1979 general election \xe2\x80\x93 was above 2,000,000 by the end of 1980. It continued to rise throughout 1981, passing the 2,500,000 mark during the summer of that year \xe2\x80\x93 although inflation was now down to 12% compared to 27% two years earlier. The economy was now in <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession" title="Early 1980s recession">recession</a>.\n</p><p>Her early tax policy reforms were based on the monetarist theories of <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Friedman</a> rather than the <a href="/wiki/Supply-side_economics" title="Supply-side economics">supply-side economics</a> of <a href="/wiki/Art_Laffer" class="mw-redirect" title="Art Laffer">Arthur Laffer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jude_Wanniski" title="Jude Wanniski">Jude Wanniski</a>, which the government of <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> espoused. There was a severe recession in the early 1980s, and the Government\'s economic policy was widely blamed. In January 1982, the inflation rate dropped to single figures and interest rates fell. Unemployment peaked at 3.1 million and remained at that level until 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>  The recession of the early 1980s was the deepest in Britain since the depression of the 1930s and Thatcher\'s popularity plummeted; most predictions had her losing the next election.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Falklands_War">Falklands War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Falklands War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg/220px-ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg/330px-ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg/440px-ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1033" data-file-height="730" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ARA_General_Belgrano_underway.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The British sank the Argentine cruiser <a href="/wiki/ARA_General_Belgrano" title="ARA General Belgrano">ARA General Belgrano</a> during the Falklands War.</div></div></div>\n<p>In Argentina, an unstable military junta was in power and keen on reversing its huge economic unpopularity. On 2 April 1982, it invaded the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a>, the only invasion of a British territory since World War II. <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Falkland_Islands" title="History of the Falkland Islands">Argentina has claimed</a> the islands since an 1830s dispute on their settlement. Thatcher sent a naval task force to recapture the Islands. The ensuing <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a> saw the swift defeat of Argentina in only a few days of fighting, resulting in a wave of patriotic enthusiasm for Thatcher personally, at a time when her popularity had been at an all-time low for a serving Prime Minister. Opinion polls showed a huge surge in Conservative support which would be sufficient to win a general election. In the end, the war probably raised the Conservative vote by about six points, according to scholarly studies of the polling data.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This "<a href="/wiki/Falklands_Factor" class="mw-redirect" title="Falklands Factor">Falklands Factor</a>", as it came to be known, was crucial to the scale of the Conservative majority in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">June 1983 general election</a>, with a fragmented Labour Party enduring its worst postwar election result, while the <a href="/wiki/SDP-Liberal_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="SDP-Liberal Alliance">SDP-Liberal Alliance</a> (created two years earlier in a pact between the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> and the new <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party (UK)</a> formed by disenchanted former Labour MP\'s) trailed Labour closely in terms of votes but won few seats.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Kong">Hong Kong</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Hong Kong">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transfer_of_sovereignty_over_Hong_Kong" class="mw-redirect" title="Transfer of sovereignty over Hong Kong">Transfer of sovereignty over Hong Kong</a></div>\n<p>China demanded the return of Hong Kong with the expiration of Britain\'s 99-year lease on most of the territory in 1997. Thatcher negotiated directly in September 1982 with that country\'s leader <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>  They agreed on the <a href="/wiki/Sino-British_Joint_Declaration" title="Sino-British Joint Declaration">Sino-British Joint Declaration over the Question of Hong Kong</a> which provided for a peaceful transfer of Hong Kong to Beijing\'s control in 15 years\' time, after which the city would be allowed to retain its "capitalistic" system for another 50 years.\n</p><p>Thatcher\'s strong opposition against communism as represented by the Soviet Union as well as the decisive military victory against Argentina, re-affirmed Britain\'s influential position on the world stage and bolstered Thatcher\'s firm leadership. In addition the economy was showing positive signs of recovery thanks mainly to substantial oil revenues from the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_disarmament">Nuclear disarmament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Nuclear disarmament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The 1983 election was also influenced by events in the opposition parties. Since their 1979 defeat, Labour was increasingly dominated by its "<a href="/wiki/Hard_left" title="Hard left">hard left</a>" that had emerged from the 1970s union militancy, and in opposition its policies had swung very sharply to the left while the Conservatives had drifted further to the <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right</a>. This drove a significant number of right wing Labour members and MPs to form a breakaway party in 1981, the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_(UK)" title="Social Democratic Party (UK)">Social Democratic Party</a>. Labour fought the election on <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">unilateral nuclear disarmament</a>, which proposed to abandon the British nuclear deterrent despite the threat from a nuclear armed Soviet Union, withdrawal from the <a href="/wiki/European_Community" class="mw-redirect" title="European Community">European Community</a>, and total reversal of Thatcher\'s economic and trade union changes. Indeed, one Labour MP, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Kaufman" title="Gerald Kaufman">Gerald Kaufman</a>, has called the party\'s 1983 manifesto "the longest suicide note in history". Consequently, upon the Labour split, there was a new centrist challenge, the <a href="/wiki/SDP-Liberal_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="SDP-Liberal Alliance">Alliance</a>, from the Social Democrats in electoral pact with the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>, to break the major parties\' dominance and win <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>. The British Electoral Study found that Alliance voters were preferentially tilted towards the Conservatives <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071108021324/http://www.unlockdemocracy.org.uk/charter88archive/pubs/voting/av_apend1.html">[2]</a>, but this possible loss of vote share by the Conservatives was more than compensated for by the <a href="/wiki/First_past_the_post" class="mw-redirect" title="First past the post">first past the post</a> electoral system, where marginal changes in vote numbers and distribution have disproportionate effects on the number of seats won.  Accordingly, despite the Alliance vote share coming very close to that of Labour and preventing an absolute majority in votes for the Conservatives, the Alliance failed to break into Parliament in significant numbers and the Conservatives were returned in a landslide.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_union_power">Trade union power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Trade union power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Thatcher was committed to reducing the power of the <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a> but, unlike the Heath government, adopted a strategy of incremental change rather than a single Act. Several unions launched <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strikes</a> that were wholly or partly aimed at damaging her politically.  The most significant of these was carried out by the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">National Union of Mineworkers</a> (NUM). However, Thatcher had made preparations long in advance for an NUM strike by building up coal stocks, and there were no cuts in electric power, unlike 1972. Police tactics during the strike concerned civil libertarians: stopping suspected strike sympathisers travelling towards coalfields when they were still long distances from them, phone tapping as evidenced by Labour\'s <a href="/wiki/Tony_Benn" title="Tony Benn">Tony Benn</a>, and a violent battle with mass pickets at Orgreave, Yorkshire. But images of massed militant miners using violence to prevent other miners from working, along with the fact that (illegally under a recent Act) the NUM had not held a national ballot to approve strike action. Scargill\'s policy of letting each region of the NUM call its own strike backfired when nine areas held ballots that resulted in majority votes against striking,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> and violence against strikebreakers escalated with time until reaching a tipping point with the <a href="/wiki/Killing_of_David_Wilkie" title="Killing of David Wilkie">killing of David Wilkie</a> (a taxi-driver who was taking a strikebreaker to work). The <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984-1985)" class="mw-redirect" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984-1985)">Miners\' Strike</a> lasted a full year, March 1984 until March 1985, before the drift of half the miners back to work forced the NUM leadership to give in without a deal. Thatcher had won a decisive victory and the unions never recovered their political power. This aborted political strike marked a turning point in UK politics: no longer could militant unions remove a democratically elected government. It also marked to beginning of a new economic and political culture in the UK based upon small government intervention in the economy and reduced dominance of the trade unions and welfare state.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Thatcher.27s_political_and_economic_philosophy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcher\'s_political_and_economic_philosophy">Thatcher\'s political and economic philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Thatcher&#039;s political and economic philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a></div>\n<p>Thatcher\'s political and economic philosophy emphasised <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free markets</a> and entrepreneurialism. Since gaining power, she had experimented in selling off a small nationalised company, the National Freight Company, to its workers, with a surprisingly large response. After the 1983 election, the Government became bolder and sold off many of the large utilities which had been in public ownership since the late 1940s. Many in the public took advantage of share offers, although many sold their shares immediately for a quick profit. The policy of <a href="/wiki/Privatisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Privatisation">privatisation</a>, while anathema to many on the left, has become synonymous with Thatcherism.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Reagan_and_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_at_Camp_David_1986.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/President_Reagan_and_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_at_Camp_David_1986.jpg/170px-President_Reagan_and_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_at_Camp_David_1986.jpg" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/President_Reagan_and_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_at_Camp_David_1986.jpg/255px-President_Reagan_and_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_at_Camp_David_1986.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/President_Reagan_and_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_at_Camp_David_1986.jpg 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="389" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Reagan_and_Prime_Minister_Margaret_Thatcher_at_Camp_David_1986.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thatcher with President Ronald Reagan, 1986.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the Cold War, Thatcher supported <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Doctrine" title="Reagan Doctrine">Ronald Reagan\'s policies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rollback" title="Rollback">Rollback</a> with the goal of reducing or ending Soviet Communist power.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>  This contrasted with the policy of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9tente" title="D\xc3\xa9tente">d\xc3\xa9tente</a> which the West had pursued during the 1970s, and caused friction with allies still wedded to the idea of d\xc3\xa9tente. US forces were permitted by Mrs. Thatcher to station nuclear <a href="/wiki/Cruise_missile" title="Cruise missile">cruise missiles</a> at British bases, arousing mass protests by the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a>. However, she later was the first Western leader to respond warmly to the rise of reformist Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>, declaring she liked him and "We can do business together" after a meeting three months before he came to power in 1985. This was a start in swinging the West back to a new d\xc3\xa9tente with the Soviet Union in his era, as it proved to be an indication that the Soviet regime\'s power was decaying. Thatcher outlasted the Cold War, which ended in 1989, and voices who share her views on it credit her with a part in the West\'s victory, by both the deterrence and d\xc3\xa9tente postures.\n</p><p>She supported the US bombing raid on <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> from bases in the UK in 1986 when other NATO allies would not. Her liking for defence ties with the United States was demonstrated in the <a href="/wiki/Westland_affair" title="Westland affair">Westland affair</a> when she acted with colleagues to prevent the helicopter manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Westland_Aircraft" title="Westland Aircraft">Westland</a>, a vital defence contractor, from linking with a European Consortium including the Italian firm <a href="/wiki/Agusta" title="Agusta">Agusta</a> in favour of a link with <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_Aircraft_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikorsky Aircraft Corporation">Sikorsky Aircraft Corporation</a> of the United States. Defence Secretary <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a>, who had pushed the European Consortium, resigned in protest at her style of leadership, and thereafter became a potential leadership challenger. Trade and Industry Secretary <a href="/wiki/Leon_Brittan" title="Leon Brittan">Leon Brittan</a> then had to resign for having ordered the leak of a confidential legal letter critical of Heseltine; Thatcher survived the crisis as her personal involvement in the leak was not proven.\n</p><p>By winning the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987 general election</a>, on the economic boom (with unemployment finally falling below 3,000,000 that spring) and against a stubbornly anti-nuclear Labour opposition (now led by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a> after <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot\'s</a> resignation four years earlier), she became the longest serving <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> since the 1820s. Most newspapers supported her \xe2\x80\x94 with the exception of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Mirror" class="mw-redirect" title="The Daily Mirror">The Daily Mirror</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> \xe2\x80\x94 and were rewarded with regular press briefings by her press secretary, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Ingham" title="Bernard Ingham">Bernard Ingham</a>.\n</p><p>She was known as "Maggie" in the popular tabloids, which inspired the well-known <a href="/wiki/Maggie_Out_protest_song" class="mw-redirect" title="Maggie Out protest song">"Maggie Out!" protest song</a>, sung throughout that period by some of her opponents. Her unpopularity on the left is evident from the lyrics of several contemporary popular songs: "Stand Down Margaret" (<a href="/wiki/The_Beat_(British_band)" title="The Beat (British band)">The Beat</a>), "Tramp the Dirt Down" (<a href="/wiki/Elvis_Costello" title="Elvis Costello">Elvis Costello</a>), "Mother Knows Best" (<a href="/wiki/Richard_Thompson_(musician)" title="Richard Thompson (musician)">Richard Thompson</a>), and "Margaret on the Guillotine" (<a href="/wiki/Morrissey" title="Morrissey">Morrissey</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many opponents believed she and her policies created a significant North-South divide from the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Channel" title="Bristol Channel">Bristol Channel</a> to <a href="/wiki/The_Wash" title="The Wash">The Wash</a>, between the "haves" in the economically dynamic south and the "have nots" in the northern rust belt.  Hard welfare reforms in her third term created an adult Employment Training system that included full-time work done for the dole plus a \xc2\xa310 top-up, on the <a href="/wiki/Workfare" title="Workfare">workfare</a> model from the US. The "Social Fund" system that placed one-off welfare payments for emergency needs under a local budgetary limit, and where possible changed them into loans, and rules for assessing jobseeking effort by the week, were breaches of social consensus unprecedented since the 1920s.\n</p><p>The sharp fall in unemployment continued. By the end of 1987, it stood at just over 2,600,000 \xe2\x80\x93 having started the year still in excess of 3,000,000. It stood at just over 2,000,000 by the end of 1988, and by the end of 1989 less than 1,700,000 were unemployed. However, total economic growth for 1989 stood at 2% \xe2\x80\x93 the lowest since 1982 \xe2\x80\x93 signalling an end to the economic boom. Several other countries had now entered recession, and fears were now rife that Britain was also on the verge of another recession.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1988, Thatcher, a trained chemist, became concerned with environmental issues, putting on the national agenda such technical issues as  <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>.   In 1990, she opened the <a href="/wiki/Hadley_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Hadley Centre">Hadley Centre</a> for climate prediction and research.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>In September 1988, at <a href="/wiki/Bruges,_Belgium" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruges, Belgium">Bruges</a>, Thatcher announced her opposition to proposals from the <a href="/wiki/European_Community" class="mw-redirect" title="European Community">European Community</a> for a federal structure and increasing centralisation of decision-making. Although she had supported British membership, Thatcher believed that the role of the EC should be limited to ensuring free trade and effective competition, and feared that new EC regulations would reverse the changes she was making in the UK. "We have not successfully rolled back the frontiers of the state in Britain, only to see them re-imposed at a European level, with a new super-state exercising a new dominance from Brussels". The speech caused an outcry from other European leaders, and exposed for the first time the deep split that was emerging over European policy inside her Conservative Party. Since 1985 Thatcher had been blocking British membership of the <a href="/wiki/Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange Rate Mechanism">Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> (ERM), a preparation for <a href="/wiki/Economic_and_Monetary_Union_of_the_European_Union" title="Economic and Monetary Union of the European Union">Economic and Monetary Union</a>, through which a single currency would replace national currencies, which the EC began seriously to discuss by 1990.\n</p><p>Thatcher\'s popularity once again declined in 1989 as the economy suffered from high interest rates imposed to stop an unsustainable <a href="/wiki/Boom_and_bust" class="mw-redirect" title="Boom and bust">boom</a>. She blamed her Chancellor, <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a>, who had exacerbated the boom by trying to keep the pound sterling low ("shadowing the Deutschmark") as a preparation for ERM membership; Thatcher claimed not to have been told of this and did not approve. At the <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a> European summit, Lawson and Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Howe" title="Geoffrey Howe">Geoffrey Howe</a> forced Thatcher to agree the circumstances under which she would join the <a href="/wiki/Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange Rate Mechanism">Exchange Rate Mechanism</a>. Thatcher took revenge on both by demoting Howe, and by listening more to her adviser Sir <a href="/wiki/Alan_Walters" title="Alan Walters">Alan Walters</a> on economic matters. Lawson resigned that October, feeling that Thatcher had undermined him.\n</p><p>That November, Thatcher was challenged for the leadership of the Conservative Party by Sir <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Meyer" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthony Meyer">Anthony Meyer</a>. As Meyer was a virtually unknown <a href="/wiki/Backbencher" title="Backbencher">backbench</a> MP, he was viewed as a <a href="/wiki/Stalking_horse" title="Stalking horse">stalking horse</a> candidate for more prominent members of the party.  Thatcher easily defeated Meyer\'s challenge, but there were 60 ballot papers either cast for Meyer or abstaining, a surprisingly large number for a sitting Prime Minister.\n</p><p>Thatcher\'s new system to replace local government rates was introduced in Scotland in 1989 and in England and Wales in 1990. Rates were replaced by the "Community Charge" (more widely known as the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">poll tax</a>), which applied the same amount to every individual resident, with only limited discounts for low earners. This was to be the most universally unpopular policy of her premiership, and saw the Conservative government split further behind the Labour opposition (still led by Neil Kinnock) in the opinion polls. The Charge was introduced early in Scotland as the rateable values would in any case have been reassessed in 1989. However, it led to accusations that Scotland was a \'testing ground\' for the tax. Thatcher apparently believed that the new tax would be popular, and had been persuaded by Scottish Conservatives to bring it in early and in one go. Despite her hopes, the early introduction led to a sharp decline in the already low support for the Conservative party in Scotland.\n</p><p>Additional problems emerged when many of the tax rates set by local councils proved to be much higher than earlier predictions.  Some have argued that local councils saw the introduction of the new system of taxation as the opportunity to make significant increases in the amount taken, assuming (correctly) that it would be the originators of the new tax system and not its local operators who would be blamed.\n</p><p>A large London demonstration against the poll tax in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> on 31 March 1990 \xe2\x80\x94 the day before it was introduced in England and Wales \xe2\x80\x94 turned into a <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_riots" title="Poll tax riots">riot</a>. Millions of people resisted paying the tax. Opponents of the tax banded together to resist <a href="/wiki/Bailiff" title="Bailiff">bailiffs</a> and disrupt court hearings of poll tax debtors. Mrs Thatcher refused to compromise, or change the tax, and its unpopularity was a major factor in Thatcher\'s downfall.\n</p><p>By the autumn of 1990, opposition to Thatcher\'s policies on local government taxation, her Government\'s perceived mishandling of the economy (especially high interest rates of 15%, which were undermining her core voting base within the home-owning, entrepreneurial and business sectors), and the divisions opening within her party over the appropriate handling of <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> made Thatcher and her party seem increasingly politically vulnerable. Her increasingly combative, irritable personality also made opposition to her grow fast and by this point, even many in her own party could not stand her.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="John_Major_.281990.E2.80.9397.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="John_Major_(1990\xe2\x80\x9397)">John Major (1990\xe2\x80\x9397)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: John Major (1990\xe2\x80\x9397)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)_leadership_election,_1990" title="Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1990">Conservative Party (UK) leadership election, 1990</a>; <a href="/wiki/First_Major_ministry" title="First Major ministry">First Major ministry</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Second_Major_ministry" title="Second Major ministry">Second Major ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Major_1996.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/John_Major_1996.jpg/220px-John_Major_1996.jpg" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/John_Major_1996.jpg/330px-John_Major_1996.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/John_Major_1996.jpg/440px-John_Major_1996.jpg 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="628" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Major_1996.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In November 1990, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Heseltine" title="Michael Heseltine">Michael Heseltine</a> challenged Margaret Thatcher for leadership of the Conservative Party. Thatcher fell short of the required 15% majority in the first round and was persuaded to withdraw from the second round on 22 November, ending her 11-year premiership. Her Chancellor of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a> contested the second round and defeated Michael Heseltine as well as Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hurd" title="Douglas Hurd">Douglas Hurd</a>, becoming prime minister on 27 November 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By this stage, however, Britain had slid into <a href="/wiki/Early_1990s" class="mw-redirect" title="Early 1990s">recession</a> for the third time in less than 20 years. Unemployment had started to rise in the spring of 1990 but by the end of the year it was still lower than in many other European economies, particularly France and Italy.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_gulf_war.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/British_gulf_war.jpg/170px-British_gulf_war.jpg" width="170" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/British_gulf_war.jpg/255px-British_gulf_war.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/British_gulf_war.jpg 2x" data-file-width="270" data-file-height="485" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_gulf_war.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British troops in the Gulf</div></div></div>\n<p>John Major was Prime Minister during British involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>. Polls improved for the Conservatives despite the recession deepening throughout 1991 and into 1992, with the economy for 1991 falling 2% and unemployment passing the 2,000,000 mark.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  Major called a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">general election</a> for April 1992 and took his campaign onto the streets, famously delivering many addresses from an upturned soapbox as in his Lambeth days. This populist "common touch", in contrast to the Labour Party\'s more slick campaign, chimed with the electorate and Major won, albeit with a small parliamentary majority.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The narrow majority for the Conservative government proved to be unmanageable, particularly after the United Kingdom\'s forced exit from the <a href="/wiki/Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange Rate Mechanism">Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> on <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a> (16 September 1992) just five months into the new parliament. From this stage onwards, Labour \xe2\x80\x93 now led by <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Labour_Party_leader)" title="John Smith (Labour Party leader)">John Smith</a> \xe2\x80\x93 was ascendant in the opinion polls. Major allowed his economic team to stay in place unchanged for seven months after Black Wednesday before forcing the resignation of his Chancellor, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lamont_(1942)" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Lamont (1942)">Norman Lamont</a>, whom he replaced with <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clarke" title="Kenneth Clarke">Kenneth Clarke</a>. This delay was seen as indicative of one of his weaknesses, an indecisiveness towards personnel issues that was to undermine his authority through the rest of his premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorey,_1999_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorey,_1999-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the 1993 Conservative Party Conference, Major began his ill-fated  "<a href="/wiki/Back_to_Basics_(campaign)" title="Back to Basics (campaign)">Back to Basics</a>" campaign, which he intended to be about the economy, education, policing, and other such issues, but it was interpreted by many (including Conservative cabinet ministers) as an attempt to revert to the moral and family values that the Conservative Party were often associated with. A number of sleaze scandals involving Conservative MP\'s were exposed in lurid and embarrassing detail in tabloid newspapers following this and further reduced the Conservative\'s popularity. Despite Major\'s best efforts, the Conservative Party collapsed into political infighting. Major took a moderate approach but found himself undermined by the right-wing within the party and the Cabinet.\n</p><p>Major\'s policy towards the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> aroused opposition as the Government attempted to ratify the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty" title="Maastricht Treaty">Maastricht Treaty</a>. Although the Labour opposition supported the treaty, they were prepared to undertake tactical moves to weaken the government, which included passing an amendment that required a vote on the social chapter aspects of the treaty before it could be ratified. Several Conservative MPs (the <a href="/wiki/Maastricht_Rebels" title="Maastricht Rebels">Maastricht Rebels</a>) voted against the Government and the vote was lost. Major hit back by calling another vote on the following day (23 July 1993), which he declared a <a href="/wiki/Vote_of_confidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Vote of confidence">vote of confidence</a>. He won by 40 but had damaged his authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Dorey,_1999_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dorey,_1999-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the few bright spots of 1993 for the Conservative government came in April when the end of the recession was finally declared after nearly three years.  Unemployment had touched 3,000,000 by the turn of the year, but had dipped to 2,800,000 by Christmas as the economic recovery continued.   The economic recovery was strong and sustained throughout 1994, with unemployment falling below 2,500,000 by the end of the year.  However, Labour remained ascendant in the opinion polls and their popularity further increased with the election of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> \xe2\x80\x93 who redesignated the party as <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a> \xe2\x80\x93 as leader following the sudden death of John Smith on 12 May 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Labour remained ascendant in the polls throughout 1995, despite the Conservative government overseeing the continuing economic recovery and fall in unemployment.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> It was a similar story throughout 1996, despite the economy still being strong and unemployment back below 2,000,000 for the first time since early 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Railways_Act_1993" title="Railways Act 1993">Railways Act 1993</a> <sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> was introduced by John Major\'s <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> government and passed on 5 November 1993. It provided for the restructuring of the <a href="/wiki/British_Railways_Board" title="British Railways Board">British Railways Board</a> (BRB), the public corporation that owned and operated the national railway system.  A few residual responsibilities of the BRB remained with <a href="/wiki/BRB_(Residuary)_Ltd." class="mw-redirect" title="BRB (Residuary) Ltd.">BRB (Residuary) Ltd.</a>\n</p><p>Few were surprised when Major lost the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1997" title="United Kingdom general election, 1997">1997 general election</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, though the immense scale of the defeat was not widely predicted. In the new parliament Labour won 418 seats, the Conservatives 165, and the Liberal Democrats 46, leaving the Labour party with a majority of 179 which was the biggest majority since <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1931" title="United Kingdom general election, 1931">1931</a>. In addition, the Conservatives lost all their seats in Scotland and Wales and several cabinet ministers including <a href="/wiki/Michael_Portillo" title="Michael Portillo">Michael Portillo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Rifkind" title="Malcolm Rifkind">Malcolm Rifkind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ian_Lang" class="mw-redirect" title="Ian Lang">Ian Lang</a> lost their seats, as did former cabinet minister <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lamont,_Baron_Lamont_of_Lerwick" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Lamont, Baron Lamont of Lerwick">Norman Lamont</a>. Major carried on as <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a> until <a href="/wiki/William_Hague" title="William Hague">William Hague</a> was elected to lead the Conservative Party the month after the election.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Labour_Government.2C_1997.E2.80.932010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_Government,_1997\xe2\x80\x932010">Labour Government, 1997\xe2\x80\x932010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Labour Government, 1997\xe2\x80\x932010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Tony_Blair_.281997.E2.80.932007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tony_Blair_(1997\xe2\x80\x932007)">Tony Blair (1997\xe2\x80\x932007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Tony Blair (1997\xe2\x80\x932007)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Tony_Blair" title="Premiership of Tony Blair">Premiership of Tony Blair</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blair_ministry" title="Blair ministry">Blair ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TonyBlairBasra.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/TonyBlairBasra.JPG/220px-TonyBlairBasra.JPG" width="220" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/TonyBlairBasra.JPG/330px-TonyBlairBasra.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/TonyBlairBasra.JPG 2x" data-file-width="408" data-file-height="593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TonyBlairBasra.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Tony Blair became Prime Minister in 1997 after a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1997" title="United Kingdom general election, 1997">landslide victory</a> over the Conservative Party. Under the title of <i><a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a></i>, he promised economic and social reform and brought Labour closer to the <a href="/wiki/Centrism" title="Centrism">centre</a> of the political spectrum. Early policies of the Blair government included the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a> and the introduction of university <a href="/wiki/Tuition_fees" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuition fees">tuition fees</a>. <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> also gave the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> the power to set the base rate of interest autonomously. The traditional tendency of governments to manipulate interest rates around the time of general elections for political gain is thought to have been deleterious to the UK economy and helped reinforce a cyclical pattern of <a href="/wiki/Boom_and_bust" class="mw-redirect" title="Boom and bust">boom and bust</a>. Brown\'s decision was popular with <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">the City</a>, which the Labour Party had been courting since the early 1990s. Blair presided over the longest period of economic expansion in Britain since the 19th century and his premiership saw large investment into social aspects, in particular health and education, areas particularly under-invested during the Conservative government of the 1980s and early 1990s. The <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_1998" title="Human Rights Act 1998">Human Rights Act</a> was introduced in 1998 and the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_2000" title="Freedom of Information Act 2000">Freedom of Information Act</a> came into force in 2000. Most hereditary peers were removed from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> in 1999 and the <a href="/wiki/Civil_partnership_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Civil partnership in the United Kingdom">Civil Partnership Act</a> of 2005 allowed <a href="/wiki/Homosexual" class="mw-redirect" title="Homosexual">homosexual</a> couples the right to register their partnership with the same rights and responsibilities comparable to heterosexual marriage.\n</p><p>The nation was stunned when Princess Diana <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Princess_Diana" class="mw-redirect" title="Death of Princess Diana">died in a car accident in Paris</a> on 31 August 1997, even though she had divorced Prince Charles a few years earlier. Numerous conspiracy theories arose about her being intentionally murdered due to her plans to marry an Arab businessman, although nothing was ever proven.\n</p><p>From the beginning, New Labour\'s record on the economy and unemployment was strong, suggesting that they could break with the trend of Labour governments overseeing an economic decline while in power. They had inherited an unemployment count of 1,700,000 from the Conservatives, and by the following year unemployment was down to 1,300,000 \xe2\x80\x93 a level not seen since <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> was in power some 20 years previously. A minimum wage was announced in May 1998, coming into force from April 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Unemployment would remain similarly low for the next 10 years.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TonyBlairArmagh1998.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/TonyBlairArmagh1998.jpg/220px-TonyBlairArmagh1998.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/TonyBlairArmagh1998.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="239" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TonyBlairArmagh1998.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blair addresses Armagh in 1998</div></div></div>\n<p>The long-running <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_peace_process" title="Northern Ireland peace process">Northern Ireland peace process</a> was brought to a conclusion in 1998 with the <a href="/wiki/Belfast_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Belfast Agreement">Belfast Agreement</a> which established a devolved <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> and de-escalated the violence associated with <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a>. It was signed in April 1998 by the British and Irish governments and was endorsed by all the main political parties in Northern Ireland with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist Party</a>. Voters in Northern Ireland approved the agreement in a referendum in May 1998 and it came into force in December 1999. In August 1998, a car-bomb <a href="/wiki/Omagh_bombing" title="Omagh bombing">exploded</a> in the Northern Ireland town of <a href="/wiki/Omagh" title="Omagh">Omagh</a>, killing 29 people and injuring 220. The attack was carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Real_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Real Irish Republican Army">Real Irish Republican Army</a> who opposed the Belfast Agreement. It was reported in 2005, that the IRA had renounced violence and had ditched its entire arsenal.\n</p><p>In foreign policy, following the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a> in the United States, Blair greatly supported <a href="/wiki/U.S._President" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. President">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>\'s new <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a> which began with the forced withdrawal of the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> regime in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>. Blair\'s case for the subsequent war in Iraq was based on their alleged possession of weapons of mass destruction and consequent violation of UN resolutions. He was wary of making direct appeals for regime change, since international law does not recognise this as a ground for war. A memorandum from a July 2002 meeting that was leaked in April 2005 showed that Blair believed that the British public would support regime change in the right political context; the document, however, stated that legal grounds for such action were weak. On 24 September 2002 the Government published a dossier based on the intelligence agencies\' assessments of <a href="/wiki/Iraq_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Iraq and weapons of mass destruction">Iraq\'s weapons of mass destruction</a>. Among the items in the dossier was a recently received intelligence report that "the Iraqi military are able to deploy chemical or biological weapons within 45 minutes of an order to do so". A further briefing paper on Iraq\'s alleged WMDs was issued to journalists in February 2003. This document was discovered to have taken a large part of its text without attribution from a PhD thesis available on the internet. Where the thesis hypothesised about possible WMDs, the Downing Street version presented the ideas as fact. The document subsequently became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Iraq_Dossier" title="Iraq Dossier">Dodgy Dossier</a>".\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg/220px-Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg/330px-Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg/440px-Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Operation_Sond_Chara_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British troops in the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001\xe2\x80\x93present)">Afghan War</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>46,000 British troops, one-third of the total strength of the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> (land forces), were deployed to assist with the invasion of Iraq. When after the war, no WMDs were found in Iraq, the two dossiers, together with Blair\'s other pre-war statements, became an issue of considerable controversy. Many Labour Party members, including a number who had supported the war, were among the critics. Successive independent inquiries (including those by the Foreign Affairs Select Committee of the House of Commons, the senior judge Lord Hutton, and the former senior civil servant Lord Butler of Brockwell) have found that Blair honestly stated what he believed to be true at the time, though Lord Butler\'s report did imply that the Government\'s presentation of the intelligence evidence had been subject to some degree of exaggeration. These findings have not prevented frequent accusations that Blair was deliberately deceitful, and, during the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 election campaign</a>, Conservative leader <a href="/wiki/Michael_Howard" title="Michael Howard">Michael Howard</a> made political capital out of the issue. The new threat of international terrorism ultimately led to the <a href="/wiki/7_July_2005_London_bombings" title="7 July 2005 London bombings">7 July 2005 bomb attacks</a> in London which killed 52 people as well as the four suicide bombers who led the attack.\n</p><p>The Labour government was re-elected with a second successive landslide in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">general election</a> of June 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Blair became the first Labour leader to lead the party to three successive election victories when they won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 general election</a>, though this time he had a drastically reduced majority.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Conservatives had so far failed to represent a serious challenge to Labour\'s rule, with John Major\'s successor William Hague unable to make any real improvement upon the disastrous 1997 general election result at the next election four years later. He stepped down after the 2001 election to be succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Iain_Duncan_Smith" title="Iain Duncan Smith">Iain Duncan Smith</a>, who did not even hold the leadership long enough to contest a general election \xe2\x80\x93 being ousted by his own MP\'s in October 2003<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and being replaced by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Howard" title="Michael Howard">Michael Howard</a>, who had served as <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> in the government of John Major. Howard failed to win the 2005 general election for the Conservatives but he at least had the satisfaction of narrowing the Labour majority, giving his successor (he announced his resignation shortly after the election) a decent platform to build upon.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> However, the Conservatives began to re-emerge as an electable prospect following the election of <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> as Howard\'s successor in December 2005. Within months of Cameron becoming Conservative leader, opinion polls during 2006 were showing a regular Conservative lead for the first time since Black Wednesday 14 years earlier. Despite the economy still being strong and unemployment remaining low, Labour\'s decline in support was largely blamed upon poor control of immigration and allowing Britain to become what was seen by many as an easy target for terrorists.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Devolution_for_Scotland_and_Wales">Devolution for Scotland and Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Devolution for Scotland and Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Blair also came into power with a policy of <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a>. A pre-legislative <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution_referendum,_1997" title="Scottish devolution referendum, 1997">referendum</a> was held in Scotland in 1997 with two questions:  whether to create a devolved <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Parliament</a> for Scotland and whether it should have limited tax-varying powers. Following a clear \'yes\' vote on both questions, a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution_referendum,_1997" title="Welsh devolution referendum, 1997">referendum</a> on the proposal for creating a devolved <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Assembly</a> was held two weeks later. This produced a narrow \'yes\' vote. Both measures were put into effect and the Scottish Parliament and Welsh Assembly began operating in 1999. The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_election,_1999" title="Scottish Parliament election, 1999">first election to the Scottish parliament</a> saw the creation of a Labour-Liberal Democrat coalition with <a href="/wiki/Donald_Dewar" title="Donald Dewar">Donald Dewar</a> as <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Scotland" title="First Minister of Scotland">First Minister</a>. In Wales, the Labour Party achieved a complete majority with <a href="/wiki/Alun_Michael" title="Alun Michael">Alun Michael</a> as the <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_for_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="First Minister for Wales">Welsh First Minister</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_Election,_2007" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Parliament Election, 2007">2007 Scottish election</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> gained enough seats to form a minority government with its leader <a href="/wiki/Alex_Salmond" title="Alex Salmond">Alex Salmond</a> as First Minister.\n</p><p>Devolution also returned to <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland</a>, leaving England as the only constituent <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">country of the United Kingdom</a> without a devolved administration. Within England, a devolved authority for <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly" title="London Assembly">London</a> was re-established following a \'yes\' vote in a London-wide referendum.\n</p><p>On 18 September 2014, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">a referendum on Scottish independence</a> failed with a 55/44 percentage.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Gordon_Brown_.282007.E2.80.9310.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gordon_Brown_(2007\xe2\x80\x9310)">Gordon Brown (2007\xe2\x80\x9310)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Gordon Brown (2007\xe2\x80\x9310)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Gordon_Brown" title="Premiership of Gordon Brown">Premiership of Gordon Brown</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brown_ministry" title="Brown ministry">Brown ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown_official.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gordon_Brown_official.jpg/220px-Gordon_Brown_official.jpg" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gordon_Brown_official.jpg/330px-Gordon_Brown_official.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Gordon_Brown_official.jpg/440px-Gordon_Brown_official.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2121" data-file-height="2711" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gordon_Brown_official.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Tony Blair tendered his resignation as Prime Minister of the United Kingdom to the Queen on 27 June 2007, his successor <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> assuming office the same afternoon. Brown took over as Prime Minister without having to face either a general election or a contested election for leadership of the Labour Party.\n</p><p>Brown\'s style of government differed from that of his predecessor, Tony Blair, who had been seen as presidential. Brown rescinded some of the policies which had either been introduced or were planned by Blair\'s administration. He remained committed to close ties with the United States and to the Iraq war, although he established an inquiry into the reasons why Britain had participated in the conflict. He proposed a "government of all the talents" which would involve co-opting leading personalities from industry and other professional walks of life into government positions. Brown also appointed <a href="/wiki/Jacqui_Smith" title="Jacqui Smith">Jacqui Smith</a> as the UK\'s first female Home Secretary, while Brown\'s old position as Chancellor was taken over by <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Darling" title="Alistair Darling">Alistair Darling</a>.\n</p><p>Brown was closer to American thinking, and more distant from Europe, than Blair. In major issues with foreign policy complications, He paid close attention to both the United States and the EU, especially regarding the deregulation of the Bank of England, the Welfare to Work program, and his response to the worldwide financial crisis at the G20 summit in London in 2009. Brown decided in 1997 to follow the American model and grant operational independence to set interest rates  to the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, rather than have the power remain with the Treasury. He explained the Bank\'s monetary policy objective "will be to deliver price stability and...to support the Government\'s economic policy."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>  Brown argued for a neoliberal policy on welfare in 1997. His goal was to move people off welfare and into actual employment. He stated: \n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We cannot build a dynamic economy unless we can unleash the potential and everyone. A welfare state that thwarts the opportunities that we need to hold the economy back. A welfare state that encourages work is not only fair but makes for greater dynamism in the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Brown\xe2\x80\x99s reaction to the great 2008 banking crisis was much more pro active than France or Germany, and in many ways resembled the Bush policies in Washington. Brown\'s goals were to provide more liquidity to the financial system, to recapitalise the banks and to guarantee bank debt. He lowered the VAT to encourage consumer spending and to keep the economy from sinking.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Brown\'s rise to prime minister sparked a brief surge in Labour support as the party topped most opinion polls. There was talk of a "snap" general election, which it was widely believed Labour could win, but Brown decided against calling an election.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Brown\'s government introduced a number of fiscal policies to help keep the British economy afloat during the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932010" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x932010">financial crisis</a> which occurred throughout the latter part of the 2000s (decade) and early 2010, although the United Kingdom saw a dramatic increase in its national debt. Unemployment soared through 2008 as the <a href="/wiki/Late_2000s_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Late 2000s recession">recession</a> set in, and Labour standings in the opinion polls plummeted as the Conservatives became ascendant.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several major banks were nationalised after falling into financial difficulties, while large amounts of money were pumped into the economy to encourage spending. Brown was also press ganged into giving <a href="/wiki/Gurkha" title="Gurkha">Gurkhas</a> settlement rights in Britain by the actress and campaigner <a href="/wiki/Joanna_Lumley" title="Joanna Lumley">Joanna Lumley</a> and attracted criticism for its handling of the <a href="/wiki/Release_of_Abdelbaset_Ali_Mohamed_Al_Megrahi" class="mw-redirect" title="Release of Abdelbaset Ali Mohamed Al Megrahi">release of Abdelbaset Al Megrahi</a>, the only person to have been convicted over the <a href="/wiki/Pan_Am_Flight_103" title="Pan Am Flight 103">1988 Lockerbie bombing</a>.\n</p><p>Further European integration was introduced under the Labour governments after 1997, including the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Amsterdam" title="Treaty of Amsterdam">Treaty of Amsterdam</a> (1997) and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nice" title="Treaty of Nice">Treaty of Nice</a> (2001). The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a> (2007)introduced many further changes.  Prominent changes included more <a href="/wiki/Qualified_majority_voting" class="mw-redirect" title="Qualified majority voting">qualified majority voting</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of Ministers</a>, increased involvement of the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> in the legislative process through extended <a href="/wiki/Codecision_procedure" class="mw-redirect" title="Codecision procedure">codecision</a> with the Council of Ministers, eliminating the <a href="/wiki/Three_pillars_of_the_European_Union" title="Three pillars of the European Union">pillar system</a> established by the Maastricht Treaty of the early 1990s and the creation of a <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_European_Council" title="President of the European Council">President of the European Council</a> with a term of two and a half years and a <a href="/wiki/High_Representative_of_the_Union_for_Foreign_Affairs_and_Security_Policy" title="High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy">High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy</a> to present a united position on EU policies. The Treaty of Lisbon will also make the Union\'s human rights charter, the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_Fundamental_Rights_of_the_European_Union" title="Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union">Charter of Fundamental Rights</a>, legally binding. The Lisbon Treaty also leads to an increase in the voting weight of the UK in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon#Council" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Council of the European Union</a> from 8.4% to 12.4%. In July 2008 the Labour government under <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> approved the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Initially, during the first four months of his premiership, Brown enjoyed a solid lead in the polls. His popularity amongst the public may be due to his handling of numerous serious events during his first few weeks as Prime Minister, including two attempted terrorist attacks in London and Glasgow at the end of June. However, between the end of 2007 and September 2008, his popularity had fallen significantly, with two contributing factors believed to be his perceived change of mind over plans to call a snap general election in October 2007, and his handling of the 10p tax rate cut in 2008, which led to allegations of weakness and dithering. His unpopularity led eight labour MPs to call for a leadership contest in September 2008, less than 15 months into his premiership. The threat of a leadership contest receded due to his perceived strong handling of the global financial crisis in October, but his popularity hit an all-time low, and his position became increasingly under threat after the May 2009 expenses scandal and Labour\'s poor results in the 2009 <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_local_elections,_2009" title="United Kingdom local elections, 2009">Local</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (United Kingdom)">European elections</a>. Brown\'s cabinet began to rebel with several key resignations in the run up to local elections in June 2009.\n</p><p>In January 2010, it was revealed that Britain\'s economy had resumed growth after a recession which had seen a record six successive quarters of economic detraction. However, it was a narrow return to growth, and it came after the other major economies had come out of recession.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a> resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Britain\'s first for 36 years \xe2\x80\x93 with the Conservative Party controlling 306 Seats, the Labour Party 258 Seats and the Liberal Democrats 57 Seats. Brown remained as prime minister while the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> negotiated with Labour and the Conservatives to form a coalition government. He announced his intention to resign on 10 May 2010 in order to help broker a Labour-Liberal Democrat deal. However, this became increasingly unlikely, and on 11 May Brown announced his resignation as Prime Minister and as Leader of the Labour Party. This paved the way for the Conservatives to return to power after 13 years.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His deputy <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Harman" title="Harriet Harman">Harriet Harman</a> became Leader of the Opposition until September 2010, when <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a> was elected Leader of the Labour Party.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Coalition_Government.2C_2010.E2.80.9315"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Coalition_Government,_2010\xe2\x80\x9315">Coalition Government, 2010\xe2\x80\x9315</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Coalition Government, 2010\xe2\x80\x9315">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron\xe2\x80\x93Clegg coalition">Cameron\xe2\x80\x93Clegg coalition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_David_Cameron" title="Premiership of David Cameron">Premiership of David Cameron</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Conservative%E2%80%93Liberal_Democrat_coalition_agreement" title="Conservative\xe2\x80\x93Liberal Democrat coalition agreement">Conservative&#8211;Liberal Democrat coalition agreement</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Cameron_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="David Cameron" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/David_Cameron_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg/200px-David_Cameron_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg" width="200" height="300" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/David_Cameron_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg/300px-David_Cameron_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/David_Cameron_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg/400px-David_Cameron_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2142" data-file-height="3216" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> (<a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a>) was Prime Minister (senior coalition partner)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Nick_Clegg_by_the_2009_budget_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Nick Clegg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Nick_Clegg_by_the_2009_budget_cropped.jpg/200px-Nick_Clegg_by_the_2009_budget_cropped.jpg" width="200" height="265" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/Nick_Clegg_by_the_2009_budget_cropped.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="266" data-file-height="353" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a> (<a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>) was <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister</a> (junior coalition partner)</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> won the 2010 general election but did not win enough seats to win an outright majority. <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, who had led the party since 2005 became Prime Minister on 11 May 2010 after the Conservatives formed a <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition government</a> with the Liberal Democrats. <a href="/wiki/Nick_Clegg" title="Nick Clegg">Nick Clegg</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a> was appointed Deputy Prime Minister and several other Liberal Democrats were given cabinet positions. Cameron promised to reduce Britain\'s spiralling budget deficit by cutting back on public service spending and by transferring more power to local authorities. He committed his government to Britain\'s continuing role in Afghanistan and stated that he hopes to remove British troops from the region by 2015. An <a href="/wiki/June_2010_United_Kingdom_budget" title="June 2010 United Kingdom budget">emergency budget</a> was prepared in June 2010 by Chancellor of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/George_Osborne" title="George Osborne">George Osborne</a> which stated that VAT will be raised to 20% and there will be a large reduction in public spending. A key Liberal Democrat policy is that of voting reform, to which a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_alternative_vote_referendum,_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom alternative vote referendum, 2011">referendum</a> took place in May 2011 on whether or not Britain should adopt a system of <a href="/wiki/Instant-runoff_voting" title="Instant-runoff voting">Alternative Vote</a> to elect MPs to Westminster. However, the proposal was rejected overwhelmingly, with 68% of voters in favour of retaining first-past-the-post.  The Liberal Democrat turnabout on tuition policy at the universities alienated their younger supporters, and the continuing weakness of the economy, despite spending cutbacks, alienated the elders.\n</p><p>In March 2011, UK, along with France and USA voted for military intervention against Gaddafi\'s Libya leading to <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">2011 military intervention in Libya</a>. Prince William married Kate Middleton on 29 April 2011 in a globally televised event much like his parents\' wedding 30 years earlier. In July 2013, the royal couple welcomed their first child, Prince George. In May 2015, they welcomed their second child, Charlotte Elizabeth Diana.
